The Eschatological Ruling of Faith: Removal from Hell
Al-Ghazali states the eschatological ruling of faith and cites the hadith of the grain of faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the afterlife ruling of Iman is removal from Hell, based on the Prophet's hadith about even a grain's weight of faith.

Core Idea
A single grain of faith prohibits eternal Hell.

Imam al-Ghazali now turns to the legal ruling (ḥukm shar‘ī) of Islam and Iman. He explains that this ruling has two dimensions: one in this world and one in the Hereafter. The most crucial is the eschatological dimension: that anyone with even the smallest measure of genuine faith will not remain in Hell forever, as confirmed by the Prophet's clear words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice that it begins by introducing the 'third discussion' and then presents two types of rulings.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two rulings
Al-Ghazali states that Islam and Iman have two rulings: eschatological (ukhrawī) and worldly (dunyawī). Focus on the eschatological ruling as it is the main subject.
The worldly ruling is not elaborated here.
3
Understand the eschatological ruling
The eschatological ruling is 'removal from the Fire' and 'prevention of eternal abiding.' Ask yourself: what does this imply about God's mercy and the fate of believers?
This is based on the hadith quoted.
4
Reflect on the prophetic hadith
The hadith states that whoever has a grain's weight of faith in their heart will be taken out of Hell. Consider how this hadith confirms the ruling.
Grain here means a small ant or a mustard seed.
5
Address a common misunderstanding
Some think this hadith means all believers must enter Hell first. But the hadith only promises removal for those who enter; it does not say all will enter.
This is clarified in the misconception section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث عن الحكم الشرعي. وللإسلام والإيمان حكمان: أخروي ودنيوي. أما الأخروي فهو الإخراج من النار، ومنع التخليد، إذ قال رسول الله: "ويخرج من النار من كان في قلبه مثقال ذرة من الإيمان".
The third discussion: concerning the legal ruling. Islam and Iman have two rulings: eschatological and worldly. As for the eschatological, it is removal from the Fire, and prevention of eternal abiding, since the Messenger of Allah said: “He will be brought out of the Fire who has in his heart a grain’s weight of faith.”
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the legal ruling (ḥukm shar‘ī) of Islam and Iman. The author divides the ruling into eschatological (ukhrawī) and worldly (dunyawī). He focuses on the eschatological: those who have even a grain’s weight (mithqāl dharratin) of faith will be removed from Hellfire and not remain there eternally, citing the well-known hadith from Bukhari and Muslim.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي
أخروي
إخراج من النار
مثقال ذرة
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All believers must enter Hell before eventual removal.
Correction: The hadith only promises removal for those who enter; it does not assert that all believers must enter Hell. Many believers may enter Paradise directly.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, who will be removed from Hell?
A) Only prophets
B) Whoever has even a grain of faith in his heart
C) Only those who never sinned
D) All Muslims regardless of faith

Key Takeaway
Even a grain's weight of true faith suffices to ultimately remove a person from Hellfire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your heart were examined today, would it contain even a grain's weight of true faith? What evidence in your life proves its presence or absence?
?
You have been promised that a grain of iman saves you from eternal Hell. Does this promise make you complacent about your sins, or does it drive you to deepen your faith? Which response is more sincere?
?
The hadith says faith is in the heart. Is your faith only on your tongue and in your actions, or does it reside genuinely in your heart? Take a moment to ask Allah to purify your heart and grant you that grain of iman.

Three Classical Views on the Definition of Iman
Enumerates the three major views on what constitutes Iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical views on what constitutes Iman and recognize that the author presents them before offering his own clarification.

Core Idea
Iman is defined by three schools: conviction, speech, or action.

Imam al-Ghazali begins by presenting the three major scholarly positions on the definition of Iman. Before he unveils his own explanation, he lays out these views clearly. This lesson introduces you to these three perspectives, setting the stage for deeper analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall structure. Notice the three views listed.
The passage is short but packs three distinct positions.
2
Identify each view
Find the three phrases: 'mere conviction', 'conviction and testimony of the tongue', and 'adds a third, actions of the limbs'. Label them View 1, View 2, View 3.
3
Note the progression
Notice how each view adds something: from inner only, to inner+speech, to inner+speech+actions.
This reflects a spectrum of inclusiveness.
4
Observe the author's intent
The author says 'we shall unveil the covering about it and say' – he is about to clarify. So he is not endorsing any view yet.
This is important to avoid misinterpreting the passage.
5
Reflect on importance
Why does defining Iman matter? It affects who is considered a believer, and thus rulings in this life and the next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلفوا في هذا الحكم على ماذا يترتب، وعبروا عنه بأن الإيمان ماذا؟ فمن قائل: إنه مجرد العقد. ومن قائل يقول: إنه عقد بالقلب وشهادة اللسان. ومن قائل يزيد ثالثاً وهو العمل بالأركان. ونحن نكشف الغطاء عنه ونقول:
And they differed concerning this ruling—upon what does it depend? And they expressed it by saying: what is Iman? Some said: it is mere conviction. Some said: it is conviction in the heart and testimony of the tongue. And some said: it adds a third, namely, actions of the limbs. And we shall unveil the covering about it and say:
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the three classical views on the definition of Iman. The first view defines Iman as mere inner conviction (ʿaqd). The second adds verbal testimony (shahādat al-lisān). The third adds outward actions (ʿamal bil-arkān). The author then announces his intention to explain and clarify these views in what follows.

Key Terms
عقد
شهادة اللسان
العمل بالأركان
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author adopts one of these views immediately within this passage.
Correction: He only lists the views and promises to clarify later; he does not endorse any at this point.

Checkpoint
How many views on the definition of Iman are mentioned here?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The definition of Iman is disputed among scholars: some define it as inner conviction, others add verbal testimony, and still others include bodily actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these three views best describes your actual practice of Iman? Does your daily life reflect mere conviction, or do your words and actions testify to your faith? Be honest: is there a gap between what you believe and what you do?
?
If Iman includes actions of the limbs, then every sin is a wound to your faith. Look at your habits: which actions are you tolerating that weaken your conviction? Will you commit to changing one of them today?
?
The author promises to 'unveil the covering' about Iman. Are you truly ready to have your understanding of faith challenged and deepened, even if it requires more than you currently give? What are you holding back from Allah right now?

The Ash'ari/Maturidi Definition of Iman as Heart's Conviction
Explains the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that Iman is heart's conviction of necessary religious truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari/Maturidi view that Iman is the heart's affirmation of necessary religious truths, and the distinction between summary and detailed belief.

Core Idea
Iman is the heart's conviction of what is known necessarily from the Prophet's religion.

Imam Ghazali now presents the first major view on the definition of Iman: that it is fundamentally a matter of the heart's affirmation. This view, associated with the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools, emphasizes internal conviction over external actions. In this passage, he explains what this conviction consists of and how it applies to different levels of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text once to get the flow, then read the English translation to understand the meaning. Note the technical terms.
Pay attention to the phrase 'عقد القلب' which is central.
2
Identify the three components of Iman
The passage says Iman is 'what the heart has contracted from affirmation, acceptance, and submission.' Understand each term and how they together form conviction.
These three are not always distinguished in everyday speech, but here they emphasize the heart's role.
3
Distinguish between summary and detailed belief
Notice that some religious truths require only general belief (like believing in all angels) while others require specific knowledge (like believing in Jibril and Mika'il by name).
This distinction shows that Iman does not require exhaustive knowledge of every detail.
4
Connect to the schools
Be aware that this definition is attributed to the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools, which represents a major position in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نكشف الغطاء عنه ونقول: أي مسمى الإيمان هو مجرد ما عقد عليه القلب من التصديق والقبول والإذعان لما علم بالضرورة أنه من دين سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم بحيث تعلمه العامة من غير افتقار إلى نظر واستدلال، كالوحدانية، والنبوة، والبعث والجزاء، ووجوب الصلاة والزكاة وحرمة الخمر ونحوها، ويكفي الإجمال فيما يلاحظ إجمالاً كالإيمان بالملائكة والكتب والرسل ويشترط التفصيل فيما يلاحظ تفصيلاً كجبريل وميكائيل وموسى وعيسى والتوراة والإنجيل، كما هو مختار الأشاعرة وبه قال الماتريديه ومشهور عن أصحابه، وعن بعض المحققين من الأشاعرة.
And we shall unveil the covering about it and say: that is, the meaning of Iman is merely what the heart has contracted from affirmation, acceptance, and submission to what is known necessarily to be from the religion of our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, such that the common people learn it without need for reflection and reasoning, like the Oneness, Prophethood, Resurrection and Recompense, the obligation of prayer and zakat, the prohibition of wine and the like. And summary is sufficient in matters that are noted summarily, like belief in angels, books, and messengers; and detail is required in matters that are noted in detail, like Jibril, Mika'il, Musa, Isa, the Torah, and the Gospel. This is the chosen view of the Ash'aris, and the Maturidis hold it, and it is the well-known view of his companions, and some investigators among the Ash'aris.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the first view (Iman as mere heart's conviction). The author defines Iman as taṣdīq (affirmation), qabūl (acceptance), and idh‘ān (submission) to what is known necessarily (ḍarūrah) from the religion of the Prophet. For common people, this does not require deep reasoning. He lists examples of beliefs that must be held in detail (e.g., specific prophets) and those where summary belief suffices (e.g., angels in general). He attributes this view to the Ash‘ari and Māturīdī schools.

Key Terms
عقد القلب
تصديق
ضروري
إجمال
تفصيل
أشاعرة
ماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this view denies the importance of speech or action.
Correction: The author only defines the essence of Iman, not its completeness or effects.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view, what is required for belief in angels?
A) Detailed knowledge of each angel's name
B) Summary belief is sufficient
C) Angels are not a required belief
D) One must believe only in Jibril

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari/Maturidi view defines Iman as the heart's affirmation of necessary religious truths, requiring detail for specific beliefs but allowing summary for general categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly have that heart conviction in the matters the passage lists—like the obligation of prayer, prohibition of wine, the resurrection—or are you simply going through routines? What evidence in your daily life shows that your heart has submitted to these truths?
?
Think of a specific religious teaching you believe in only generally, like belief in angels. Have you ever taken the time to affirm individual angels like Jibril or Mika'il with detailed conviction? If not, why do you settle for summary belief when the Prophet taught these details?
?
When you hear the names of prophets like Musa or Isa, does your heart affirm them with the same certainty as your belief in the oneness of God? If there is any hesitation or indifference, what does that reveal about the completeness of your Iman according to this definition?

The Khawarij View: Actions as Integral to Iman
Imam al-Ghazali presents the Khawarij definition of Iman and its consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij position that Iman consists of three inseparable components and why they consider major sins as expelling one from faith entirely.

Core Idea
For the Khawarij, sin negates faith because actions are part of Iman's very essence.

The Khawarij took an extreme position on the relationship between faith and action. They argued that Iman is not merely belief and speech but also includes physical deeds. Therefore, committing a sin—even a minor one—destroys the integrity of Iman, leaving a person no middle ground between faith and disbelief. This lesson unpacks their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the three components of Iman according to the Khawarij.
The Khawarij added 'action of the limbs' as a necessary part.
2
Understand their definition of Iman
Identify the three parts: tasdiq bil-qalb, iqrar bil-lisan, and amal bil-jawarih. Recognize that all three are required for Iman.
3
Trace the consequence
Note that losing any part means losing the whole essence, so a sinner is kāfir. Understand their reasoning that there is no intermediate state (lā wāsiṭah).
This is the key difference from the Murji'ah and Ahl al-Sunnah.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Reflect on the fact that the Khawarij are a minority position, not the mainstream Islamic view.
Ahl al-Sunnah hold that sins do not negate Iman, though they may decrease its perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمراً ثالثاً وهو العمل بالأركان، أي سائر الجوارح، وهذا قول الخوارج، فمسمى الإيمان عندهم تصديق القلب، والإقرار باللسان والعمل بالجوارح، فماهيته على هذا مركبة من ثلاثة: فمن أخل بشيء منها فهو كافر، ولذا قالوا: مرتكب الذنب مطلقا كافر، لانتفاء جزء الماهية، والذنوب عندهم كبائر كلها، وتعليلهم بانتفاء جزء الماهية مبني على أن لا واسطة بين الإيمان والكفر.
A third, which is action of the limbs, i.e., all the bodily parts, and this is the statement of the Khawarij. So the meaning of Iman according to them is: affirmation of the heart, acknowledgment by the tongue, and action of the limbs. So its essence on this is composed of three: whoever neglects anything from them is a disbeliever. Hence they said: the committer of a sin absolutely is a disbeliever, due to the non-existence of a part of the essence. And sins according to them are all major. And their reasoning by the non-existence of a part of the essence is built on there being no intermediate station between faith and disbelief.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage explains the Khawarij position. They define Iman as three components: heart's affirmation, tongue's acknowledgment, and bodily actions. Missing any one renders a person a disbeliever (kāfir). They consider all sins as major, and since any sin violates the action component, it negates Iman entirely. This follows from their denial of any intermediate state (lā wāsiṭah) between Iman and kufr.

Key Terms
خوارج
عمل
جوارح
كافر
ذنب
واسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij view that major sins expel one from faith is the mainstream Islamic position.
Correction: The text presents this as a specific sectarian view (the Khawarij), and Ahl al-Sunnah reject it, holding that faith is not annulled by sins.

Checkpoint
According to the Khawarij, what is the status of a person who commits a major sin?
A) Believer with incomplete faith
B) Disbeliever
C) In a middle station between faith and disbelief
D) Still a believer but a sinner

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij equated Iman with a composite of belief, speech, and action, thereby declaring all sinners disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim openly committing a sin, do you catch yourself questioning their faith? This lesson shows an extreme view; where in your heart do you draw the line between sin and faith? Commit to reminding yourself that Iman is affirmed by belief and speech, and that actions can be deficient without removing Iman entirely.
?
The Khawarij rejected any intermediate state: for them you are either fully a believer or fully a disbeliever. In your own judgment of others' religiosity, do you ever fall into a similar binary thinking? How can you actively cultivate the Ahl al-Sunnah view of degrees of Iman?
?
This lesson forces you to confront the consequence of defining Iman too narrowly. Have you ever defined someone's Islam based solely on their outward actions? What would you need to change in your own perspective to avoid the Khawarij error?

The Mu'tazila Intermediate Station and the Status of Grave Sinners
First difference: sins divided, fasiq in middle station.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of the intermediate station (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) for the grave sinner and how it differs from the Khawarij view.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila placed the grave sinner in a middle category: neither believer nor disbeliever.

This passage introduces the Mu'tazila position on the status of the Muslim who commits major sins. Unlike the Khawarij who declare such persons disbelievers, the Mu'tazila argue for an intermediate station. They also divide sins into major and minor, making the commission of a major sin an act of transgression (fisq) that removes one from faith but not into outright disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: الواسطة, فسق, منزلة بين المنزلتين.
The Mu'tazila coin the term 'manzila bayna al-manzilatayn'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's stance
Find the two main claims: (1) The negation of Islam does not entail kufr, (2) The fasiq is neither believer nor disbeliever but in a middle station.
They also divide sins into major and minor.
3
Contrast with Khawarij
The Khawarij would consider the fasiq a disbeliever. The Mu'tazila differ by affirming an intermediate status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما على ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة من إثبات الواسطة، فلا يلزم عندهم من انتفاء الإسلام ثبوت الكفر، وإن وافقوا الخوارج في اعتبار الأعمال فإنهم يخالفونهم من وجهين: أحدهما: أن المعتزلة يقسمون الذنوب إلى كبائر وصغائر، وارتكاب الكبيرة عندهم فسق والفاسق عندهم ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر، بل منزلة بين المنزلتين. والثاني: أن الطاعات عند الخوارج جزء كانت فرضاً أو نفلاً. وعن المعتزلة: الطاعات شرط لصحة الإيمان. ثم اختلفوا فقال ابو الهزيل العلاف وعبد الجبار: الشرط الطاعات فرضاً كانت أو نفلاً. وقال الجبائي وابنه وأكثر معتزلة البصرة الشرط هو الطاعات المفترضة من الأفعال والتروك دون النوافل.
As for what the Mu'tazila have gone to regarding affirming the intermediate station, then they do not necessarily hold that the negation of Islam entails the confirmation of disbelief. And though they agree with the Khawarij in considering actions, they differ from them in two respects: first, the Mu'tazila divide sins into major and minor, and committing a major sin according to them is fisq (transgression), and the fasiq according to them is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but a station between the two stations. Second, actions according to the Khawarij are part (whether obligatory or supererogatory). And according to the Mu'tazila, actions are a condition for the validity of faith. Then they differed: Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf and 'Abd al-Jabbar said: the condition is actions, whether obligatory or supererogatory. And al-Jubba'i and his son and most of the Basran Mu'tazila said: the condition is the obligatory actions of deeds and abstentions, not the supererogatory.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila position in contrast to the Khawarij. The Mu'tazila affirm an intermediate station (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) for the grave sinner (fāsiq), who is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. They agree with the Khawarij that actions are important, but for them actions are a condition (sharṭ) for the validity of faith, not a part. They also divide sins into major and minor. There is internal disagreement among Mu'tazila on whether supererogatory acts are part of the condition: some include them, others restrict it to obligatory acts only.

Key Terms
معتزلة
واسطة
منزلة بين المنزلتين
فسق
فاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila view on grave sinners is the same as the Khawarij view.
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm an intermediate status (neither believer nor disbeliever), while the Khawarij consider such a person a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the status of a person who commits a major sin?
A) Believer
B) Disbeliever
C) Neither believer nor disbeliever, in an intermediate station
D) A believer but with deficient faith

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila introduce an intermediate status for the grave sinner (fāsiq), distinct from both the believer and the disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim commit a major sin, do you silently place them in the category of 'disbeliever' or 'sinner but still a believer' after learning this lesson? Which category does Ibn 'Umar's heart lean toward, and how will you change your inner judgment from now on?
?
The Mu'tazila introduced a middle station so that believers would not rush to excommunicate. Is there a specific person in your life whom you have mentally excommunicated because of a sin? Will you now pray for them as a fasiq in need of guidance rather than treat them as an outsider?
?
This lesson shows that even within a deviant sect, Allah preserved some mercy in their theology—the intermediate station. If a group known for error can show restraint in judging others, how much more should you, as a follower of Ahl al-Sunnah, be careful in your tongue and heart? What is one practical step you will take this week to avoid hastily judging someone's faith?

Actions as Condition for Faith: Mu'tazila and Khawarij Views
Second difference: actions as condition, internal split on scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second difference between Mu'tazila and Khawarij—actions as condition versus part of faith—and the internal Mu'tazila disagreement on whether supererogatory acts are included.

Core Idea
For Mu'tazila, actions are a condition for valid faith, not a part; but they disagreed on the scope.

This passage continues the comparison, focusing on the role of actions. The Khawarij consider actions as part (juz') of faith, while the Mu'tazila treat them as a condition (shart) for its validity. However, the Mu'tazila themselves split into two camps: some include all acts, obligatory and supererogatory, while others restrict the condition to obligatory acts only.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second half of the source about actions as condition. Monitor the internal disagreement among Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the Khawarij and Mu'tazila views
Khawarij say actions are part (juz') of faith; Mu'tazila say they are condition (shart). Note the difference.
3
Learn the internal Mu'tazila split
Two opinions: those who include supererogatory acts (Abu al-Hudhayl, Abd al-Jabbar) and those who restrict to obligatory acts (al-Jubba'i, his son, most Basran Mu'tazila).
This shows the Mu'tazila were not monolithic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما على ما ذهب إليه المعتزلة من إثبات الواسطة، فلا يلزم عندهم من انتفاء الإسلام ثبوت الكفر، وإن وافقوا الخوارج في اعتبار الأعمال فإنهم يخالفونهم من وجهين: أحدهما: أن المعتزلة يقسمون الذنوب إلى كبائر وصغائر، وارتكاب الكبيرة عندهم فسق والفاسق عندهم ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر، بل منزلة بين المنزلتين. والثاني: أن الطاعات عند الخوارج جزء كانت فرضاً أو نفلاً. وعن المعتزلة: الطاعات شرط لصحة الإيمان. ثم اختلفوا فقال ابو الهزيل العلاف وعبد الجبار: الشرط الطاعات فرضاً كانت أو نفلاً. وقال الجبائي وابنه وأكثر معتزلة البصرة الشرط هو الطاعات المفترضة من الأفعال والتروك دون النوافل.
As for what the Mu'tazila have gone to regarding affirming the intermediate station, then they do not necessarily hold that the negation of Islam entails the confirmation of disbelief. And though they agree with the Khawarij in considering actions, they differ from them in two respects: first, the Mu'tazila divide sins into major and minor, and committing a major sin according to them is fisq (transgression), and the fasiq according to them is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but a station between the two stations. Second, actions according to the Khawarij are part (whether obligatory or supererogatory). And according to the Mu'tazila, actions are a condition for the validity of faith. Then they differed: Abu al-Hudhayl al-'Allaf and 'Abd al-Jabbar said: the condition is actions, whether obligatory or supererogatory. And al-Jubba'i and his son and most of the Basran Mu'tazila said: the condition is the obligatory actions of deeds and abstentions, not the supererogatory.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila position in contrast to the Khawarij. The Mu'tazila affirm an intermediate station (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn) for the grave sinner (fāsiq), who is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. They agree with the Khawarij that actions are important, but for them actions are a condition (sharṭ) for the validity of faith, not a part. They also divide sins into major and minor. There is internal disagreement among Mu'tazila on whether supererogatory acts are part of the condition: some include them, others restrict it to obligatory acts only.

Key Terms
شرط
فرض
نفل
خوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agreed on the scope of the condition.
Correction: The passage shows significant internal disagreement: some include supererogatory acts, others restrict to obligatory acts.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, which scholars held that supererogatory acts are part of the condition for faith?
A) Abu al-Hudhayl and 'Abd al-Jabbar
B) Al-Jubba'i and his son
C) Most Basran Mu'tazila
D) All Mu'tazila agreed

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila hold that actions are a condition for faith's validity, not a part of it; they disagreed whether supererogatory acts are included.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily practice, do you treat your obligatory acts like prayer and fasting as mere conditions to avoid punishment, or as integral parts of your faith? How would your life change if you saw them as conditions that validate your faith itself?
?
The Mu'tazila split over whether supererogatory acts are required for faith's validity. Where do you stand in your own heart? Are you content with just the minimum obligations, or do you feel your faith demands you go further? What does your answer reveal about your view of Allah's rights upon you?
?
Consider the Khawarij position: actions are part of faith. Though you likely reject it, have you ever acted as if your salvation depends on every good deed? How does distinguishing part from condition free you from despair or arrogance, and what responsibility does it place on you to maintain your faith's validity?

The Ash‘ari Definition of Iman: Inner Conviction
Ghazali defines iman as inner conviction according to the Ash‘aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘ari definition of iman as mere inner conviction and the details of what that conviction entails.

Core Idea
Iman is the heart's assent to necessary religious truths.

The passage begins by presenting three definitions of faith. The first, held by the Ash‘aris and Maturidis, defines iman as simply what the heart has assented to. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the Ash‘ari position.
2
Understand what 'inner conviction' means
Note that it includes affirmation, acceptance, and submission to necessarily known religious truths.
The passage lists examples like tawhid, prophethood, etc.
3
Differentiate general from detailed belief
See how the Ash‘aris distinguish between matters that require general belief and those requiring detail.
For example, belief in angels in general vs. knowing Jibril by name.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن قائل : إنه مجرد العقد (۱)
ومن قائل يقول : إنه عقد بالقلب وشهادة اللسان (۴)
ومن قائل يزيد (۳) ثالثاً وهو العمل بالأركان
ونحن نكشف الغطاء عنه ونقول :
(۱) أي مسمى الإيمان هو مجرد ما عقد عليه القلب من التصديق والقبول والإذعان لما علم بالضرورة أنه من دين سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم بحيث تعلمه العامة من غير إفتقار إلى نظر وإستدلال ، كالوحدانية ، والنبوة ، والبعث والجزاء ، ووجوب الصلاة والزكاة وحرمة الخمر ونحوها ، ويكفي الاجمال فيما يلاحظ إجمالاً كالإيمان بالملائكة والكتب والرسل ويشترط التفصل فيها يلاحظ تفصيلا كجبريل وميكائيل وموسى وعيسى والتوراة والإنجيل ، كما هو مختار الأشاعرة وبه قال الماتريديه ومشهور عن أصحابه ، وعن بعض
(۲) والمراد بالشهادة الإقرار وهو منقول عن الإمام أبي حنيفة
المحققين من الأشاعرة .
(۳) يزيد على التصديق ، والإقرار
أمراً ثالثاً وهو العمل الإركان وهو العمل الإركان، أي سائر الجوارح ، وهذا قول
الخوارج ، فمسمى الإيمان عندهم تصديق القلب ، والإقرار باللسان والعمل بالجوارح ، فماهيته على هذا مركبة من ثلاثة : فمن أخل بشيء منها فهو كافر ، ولذا قالوا : مرتكب الذنب مطلقا كافر ، لإنتفاء جزء الماهية ، والذنوب عندهم كبائر كلها ، وتعليلهم بإنتفاء جزء الماهية مبني على أن لا واسطة بين الإيمان والكفر
Among them are those who say: It (faith) is merely inner conviction (al-‘aqd) (1). And among them are those who say: It is inner conviction by the heart and testimony of the tongue (4). And among them are those who add (3) a third, which is action with the limbs. And we remove the covering from it and say: (1) That is, the meaning of faith is merely what the heart has assented to of affirmation, acceptance, and submission to what is necessarily known to be from the religion of our master Muhammad (peace be upon him), in a way that the common people know it without needing reflection and reasoning, such as [belief in] divine oneness, prophethood, resurrection and recompense, the obligation of prayer and zakat, the prohibition of wine and the like. And general [belief] suffices in what is observed generally, like belief in angels, books, and messengers, and detailed [belief] is required in what is observed in detail, like Jibril, Mikail, Musa, Isa, the Torah, and the Gospel. This is the choice of the Ash‘aris, and the Maturidis and the well-known [view] from their adherents hold it, and some of the verifying scholars of the Ash‘aris. (2) And what is meant by testimony is confession, and it is transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifa. (3) He adds to affirmation and confession a third thing, which is action with the limbs, i.e., the other bodily members. And this is the saying of the Khawarij. For them, the meaning of faith is affirmation of the heart, confession by the tongue, and action by the limbs. Its essence according to this is composed of three: whoever omits any of them is a disbeliever. Therefore they say: the committer of sin absolutely is a disbeliever, because of the absence of a part of the essence, and sins in their view are all major sins. And their reasoning by the absence of a part of the essence is based on there being no intermediate between faith and disbelief.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage classifies the three major definitions of iman (faith) in Islamic theology. Ghazali first states the three opinions: (1) iman is mere inner conviction (al-‘aqd), (2) iman is inner conviction plus verbal confession, (3) iman includes actions. Then he elaborates each. For the first opinion, which is the Ash‘ari and Maturidi view, he explains that iman is the heart’s assent to necessarily known religious matters, with general belief sufficient for general matters and detailed belief required for detailed matters. He notes this is the chosen view of the Ash‘aris and also held by Maturidis and many of their followers. For the second opinion, attributed to Abu Hanifa, he states that verbal confession is required. For the third opinion, that of the Khawarij, he explains that iman consists of three parts: heart’s belief, verbal confession, and bodily actions. Therefore, omitting any part makes one a disbeliever, and all sins are considered major sins. Their reasoning is that there is no intermediate state between iman and kufr.

Key Terms
إيمان
عقد
تصديق
أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ash‘ari definition excludes any form of knowledge or affirmation beyond a vague assent.
Correction: The passage shows that inner conviction includes specific affirmation of necessary truths, with detail required for detailed matters.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash‘ari view, what suffices for belief in angels?
A) Detailed knowledge of each angel
B) General belief in angels
C) Belief only in Jibril
D) Rejection of angelic names

Key Takeaway
For Ash‘aris, iman is strictly the heart's assent to necessary religious truths, without requiring verbal confession or actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe in Allah,' do you only mean intellectual agreement, or does your heart truly submit and accept? What evidence in your life shows that your iman is more than just mental assent?
?
The Ash‘aris say iman requires accepting truths 'necessarily known.' Are there fundamental truths of Islam that you have accepted casually without heartfelt conviction? How can you move from passive knowledge to active inner certainty?
?
If iman is purely inner conviction, does your heart's condition match what you claim to believe? When was the last time you honestly examined whether your inner state aligns with the truths you profess?

The Hanafi Definition: Adding Verbal Confession
Ghazali presents the Hanafi definition of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi definition of iman as inner conviction plus verbal confession.

Core Idea
Iman is conviction of the heart and testimony of the tongue.

The second definition of iman, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, adds a verbal component to the inner conviction. This lesson examines that addition and its basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
The passage is brief, stating the second opinion and its attribution.
2
Identify the two components
Note that the definition includes both heart conviction and tongue testimony.
Testimony is explained as 'iqrar' (confession).
3
Consider the source
The passage quotes this from Imam Abu Hanifa, indicating its Hanafi origin.

The Khariji Definition: Including Actions
Ghazali defines iman according to the Khawarij.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khariji definition of iman as including actions and its implications.

Core Idea
Iman for the Khawarij consists of heart, tongue, and limbs.

The third definition, held by the Khawarij, adds bodily actions to heart belief and verbal confession. This lesson explores their view that sin nullifies iman and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
It presents the third opinion and explains its consequences.
2
Note the three components
The Khawarij require affirmation, confession, and action.
3
Understand the ruling on sin
They consider any sin as kufr because it omits a part of iman.
They see no intermediate state between faith and disbelief.

?

?
The Khawarij denied any intermediate state between iman and kufr. Have you ever fallen into an 'all or nothing' mentality regarding your own faith? How can the mainstream Sunni view of a middle ground (with sin but still believer) give you hope and motivate you to repent rather than despair?

The Mu‘tazila View on Iman and Their Differences from the Khawarij
Ghazali explains the Mu‘tazila position on iman, the intermediate station, and their disagreement with the Khawarij.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu‘tazila concept of the intermediate station (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) and how their view on the role of actions in faith differs from that of the Khawarij.

Core Idea
The Mu‘tazila place the major sinner in a middle station, neither believer nor disbeliever.

This passage from Imam Ghazali presents the Mu‘tazila position on faith and their key disagreements with the Khawarij. While both groups emphasize the importance of actions, the Mu‘tazila introduce a nuanced intermediate category for the major sinner and treat actions as a condition rather than an integral part of faith. Pay close attention to the two specific differences mentioned, as well as the internal debate among Mu‘tazila scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two differences
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Note that the Mu‘tazila agree with the Khawarij that actions matter, but they differ in two distinct ways. Underline or highlight each difference as you find them.
The passage also includes an internal dispute among the Mu‘tazila – be sure to distinguish that from the two differences with the Khawarij.
2
Understand the intermediate station
Focus on the first difference: the Mu‘tazila divide sins into major and minor, and the committer of a major sin is not a believer or disbeliever, but a fasiq in a station between the two. Reflect on why this concept is called 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn'.
This is a distinctive Mu‘tazila doctrine that separates them from both the Khawarij and mainstream Sunnis.
3
Compare the role of actions: part vs. condition
The second difference concerns how actions relate to iman. The Khawarij consider actions a part (juz') of faith, while the Mu‘tazila consider them a condition (shart) for its validity. Grasp the logical difference between a part and a condition.
A condition is external and necessary for validity, whereas a part is intrinsic to the definition.
4
Explore the internal Mu‘tazila dispute
After stating the two differences, Ghazali notes that the Mu‘tazila themselves disagreed: some said all acts (obligatory and supererogatory) are conditions; others said only obligatory acts. Consider why this debate matters for their overall theory.
This shows that even within a theological school, there can be significant variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما على ما مذهب إليه المعتزلة من إثبات الواسطة ، فلا يلزم عندهم من إنتفاء الإسلام ثبوت الكفر ، وإن وافقوا الخوارج في إعتبار الأعمال فإنهم يخالفونهم من وجهين : أحدهما : أن المعتزلة يقسمون الذنوب إلى كبائر وصغائر ، وإرتكاب الكبيرة عندهم فسق والفاسق عندهم ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ، بل منزلة بين المنزلتين
والثاني : أن الطاعات عند الخوارج جزء كانت فرضاً أو نقلاً
وعن المعتزلة : الطاعات شرط لصحة الإيمان
ثم اختلفوا فقال ابو الهزيل العلاف وعبد الجبار : الشرط الطاعات قرضاً كانت أو نفلاً وقال الجبائي وابنه وأكثر معتزلة البصرة الشرط هو الطاعات المفترضة من الأفعال والتروك دون النوافل .
As for the view to which the Mu‘tazila adhere of affirming the intermediate [state], then in their view the absence of Islam does not necessitate the establishment of disbelief. And even if they agree with the Khawarij in considering actions, they differ from them in two respects: One of them: that the Mu‘tazila divide sins into major and minor, and committing a major sin in their view is fisq (immorality), and the fasiq (immoral person) in their view is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but rather a station between the two stations. And the second: that actions in the view of the Khawarij are a part [of faith], whether obligatory or supererogatory. And [according to] the Mu‘tazila: actions are a condition for the validity of faith. Then they differed: Abu al-Hudhayl al-‘Allaf and ‘Abd al-Jabbar said: the condition is actions, whether obligatory or supererogatory. And al-Jubba’i and his son and most of the Mu‘tazila of Basra said: the condition is the obligatory actions from deeds and abstentions, excluding supererogatory acts.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu‘tazila view on faith and details their differences from the Khawarij. The Mu‘tazila affirm an intermediate state (al-wasita) between iman and kufr, unlike the Khawarij who hold that iman is all-or-nothing. They agree with the Khawarij that actions are relevant to faith, but they differ in two ways. First, the Mu‘tazila classify sins into major (kaba’ir) and minor (sagha’ir), and a person who commits a major sin is not a disbeliever but a fasiq in an intermediate station (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). Second, while the Khawarij consider actions as an integral part (juz’) of the essence of iman, the Mu‘tazila regard actions as a condition (shart) for the validity of iman, not a part. Then Ghazali notes an internal Mu‘tazila disagreement: some, like Abu al-Hudhayl and ‘Abd al-Jabbar, say the condition includes all acts (obligatory and supererogatory), while others, like al-Jubba’i and his son and most Basran Mu‘tazila, say it is limited to obligatory acts and abstentions only.

منزلة بين المنزلتين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think the Mu‘tazila consider a major sinner as a believer or disbeliever.
Correction: In fact, the Mu‘tazila consider such a person a fasiq in an intermediate station (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), neither believer nor disbeliever.

A) He is a believer
B) He is a disbeliever
C) He is a fasiq in a station between belief and disbelief
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
The Mu‘tazila introduce an intermediate station for the major sinner and treat actions as a condition, not a part, of faith—distinct from both the Khawarij and mainstream Sunni views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you tend to write yourself off as a 'disbeliever' (like the Khawarij) or assume your faith is intact regardless (like a lax Muslim)? How does the Mu'tazila's intermediate station challenge your default reaction, and what does that reveal about your true understanding of God's mercy and justice?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that actions are a condition for the validity of faith, not a part of its definition. In your own life, do you treat your prayers and good deeds as optional extras or as non-negotiable conditions for your faith to be real? Be honest: when you miss an obligation, do you feel your faith is weakened, or do you compartmentalize it?
?
The Mu'tazila themselves disagreed on whether supererogatory acts are required for faith's validity. This shows that even among scholars, there is room for nuance. But for you personally, what is the one area of your religious practice that you know you neglect, yet you tell yourself it doesn't affect your faith? What would it mean to you today to treat that act as a condition rather than an option?

Three Degrees of People Concerning Iman
Ghazali enumerates three degrees of people regarding iman and introduces the Mu‘tazila position on the second degree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of people into three degrees based on the components of iman and his rejection of the Mu'tazila position on the second degree.

Core Idea
Faith is not diminished by sin; the Mu‘tazila error is an intermediate category.

Ghazali divides people by their possession of the three components of iman: inner conviction, verbal confession, and actions. He presents three degrees, culminating in a critique of the Mu‘tazila doctrine of the intermediate station. This passage lays the groundwork for a deeper discussion on sin and faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three distinct degrees mentioned.
The passage includes footnote markers; ignore them for now.
2
Identify the first degree
Find the description of the first degree: complete possession of all three components, guaranteeing Paradise. This is undisputed.
3
Examine the second degree and Mu‘tazila view
The second degree involves belief and speech plus some actions, but the person commits a major sin. The Mu‘tazila deem this person a fasiq in an intermediate station, destined for eternal Hell. Ghazali labels this false and promises a refutation.
This is the key controversial point.
4
Note the third degree and its ambiguity
The third degree is inner conviction and verbal confession without actions. Scholars differed on its ruling, as Ghazali indicates by citing Abu Talib al-Makki. This shows that actions are debated as a condition of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من جمع بين هذه الثلاثة فلا خلاف في أن مستقرة الجنة : وهذه درجة
والدرجة الثانية : أن يوجد اثنان وبعض الثالث ، وهو القول والعقد وبعض
الأعمال ، ولكن ارتكب صاحبه كبيرة أو بعض الكبائر (١) فعند هذا قالت للمعتزلة :
خرج بهذا عن الإيمان ولم يدخل في الفكر ، بل اسمه فاسق ، وهو على منزلة بين المنزلتين(٢) ، وهو مخلد في النار وهذا باطل كما سنذكره . الدرجة الثالثة : أن يوجد التصديق بالقلب والشهادة باللسان دون الأعمال
بالجوارح .
وقد اختلفوا في حكمه ، فقال أبو طالب المكي : (٣)
Whoever combines these three [components] – there is no dispute that his final abode is Paradise: that is the first degree. The second degree: that two exist and part of the third, i.e., speech and conviction and some actions, but its perpetrator committed a major sin or some major sins (1). So according to this, the Mu‘tazila said: he has exited from faith by this and has not entered into disbelief, but his name is fasiq, and he is in a station between the two stations (2), and he is immortal in the Fire. And this is false, as we will mention. The third degree: that affirmation by the heart and testimony by the tongue exist without actions by the limbs. And they differed concerning its ruling, and Abu Talib al-Makki said: (3)
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage classifies people into three degrees based on their possession of the components of iman (inner conviction, verbal confession, and actions). The first degree is the complete combination of all three, which guarantees Paradise without dispute. The second degree consists of having two components (belief and confession) plus some actions, but the person commits a major sin. In this case, the Mu‘tazila hold that such a person is no longer a believer but not a disbeliever either; he is a fasiq in an intermediate station (manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) and will abide eternally in Hell. Ghazali indicates that this Mu‘tazila position is false and will be refuted later. The third degree is having only inner conviction and verbal confession without any actions. Ghazali notes that scholars differed on the ruling of such a person, and he cites Abu Talib al-Makki, though the passage does not provide his view here.

فاسق
منزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu‘tazila believe that a Muslim who commits a major sin becomes neither a believer nor a disbeliever but a fasiq in an intermediate station, destined for eternal hellfire.
Correction: Ghazali rejects this, indicating that such a person remains a believer, as will be argued later. The intermediate station is false.

Checkpoint

A) He is a believer
B) He is a disbeliever
C) He is a fasiq in an intermediate station and will be punished eternally
D) He is a believer but will be punished temporarily

Key Takeaway
Ghazali outlines three degrees of iman and rejects the Mu‘tazila intermediate station, affirming that major sins do not remove one from faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you truly believe that your faith remains intact, or does a part of you fear that you have stepped out of iman? How would Ghazali's rejection of the Mu'tazila position change your response to your own sins?
?
Think of a person you know who openly commits sins but still professes faith. Do you secretly place them in a 'middle station' in your heart, doubting their ultimate salvation? How does this lesson challenge you to correct that judgment?
?
If you were to die tonight, which of the three degrees would you place yourself in? Based on Ghazali's classification, what missing component—conviction, confession, or actions—do you need to strengthen starting tomorrow?

The Definition of Major Sins
Ghazali gives the preferred definition of a major sin with two criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly definition of a major sin (kabira) and the two criteria used to identify it.

Core Idea
A major sin is any act showing disregard for religion or carrying a specific divine threat.

The concept of major sins (kaba'ir) is central to Islamic ethics. Scholars debated its definition. Imam Ghazali presents the best view, focusing on two indicators: the sinner's attitude and the presence of a specific warning in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the phrase 'أحسن ما قيل' indicating the preferred definition.
2
Identify the two criteria
The definition has two parts connected by 'أو'. Identify each: (1) disobedience indicating lack of concern for religion, (2) any act with a specific threat in Quran or Sunnah.
These are alternative criteria – either one qualifies as a major sin.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this definition differs from minor sins and why both criteria are needed to cover all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) واختلف في حد الكبيرة ، وعد الكبائر ، وأحسن ما قيل في حدها هي كل معصية تؤذن بقلة إكتراث مرتكبها بالدين ورقة الديانة ، أو كل ما توعد عليه بخصوصه من الكتاب أو السنة
وأما عد الكبائر : فقد قال الشيخ أبو طالب المكي هي :
أربع من أعمال القلوب : الشرك ، والإصرار ، والقنوط ، والأمن
وأربع في اللسان : شهادة الزور ، وقذف المحصنات ، واليمين الغموس ، والسحر وثلاث في البطن : شرب الخمر والمسكر من الأشربة ، وأكل مال اليتيم ، وأكل الربا وهو يعلمه
وإثنان في الفرج : الزنا ، واللواط ؛
وإثنان في اليد : القتل ، والسرقة
وواحدة في الرجل : فرار الواحد من الإثنين يوم الزحف
وواحدة في الجسد : وهي « عقوق الوالدين » اهـ
(1) And they differed concerning the definition of a major sin (al-kabira) and the enumeration of major sins. The best that has been said concerning its definition is: every disobedience that indicates the lack of concern of its perpetrator for religion and the weakness of religiosity, or every [act] for which there is a specific threat in the Book or the Sunnah. As for the enumeration of major sins: al-Shaykh Abu Talib al-Makki said they are: Four from the works of the hearts: shirk (associating partners with God), insistence [on sin], despair (qunut) [of God's mercy], and security [from God's plan]. And four on the tongue: false testimony, slandering chaste women, the false oath (al-yamin al-ghamus), and sorcery. And three in the stomach: drinking wine and intoxicating drinks, consuming the wealth of orphans, and consuming usury while knowing [it is prohibited]. And two in the private parts: adultery and sodomy. And two in the hand: murder and theft. And one in the foot: fleeing from two [enemies] on the day of battle. And one in the body: which is disobedience to parents. End.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition and enumeration of major sins (kaba’ir). It begins by stating that scholars differed on the definition, and it provides the best view: a major sin is any act of disobedience that reflects a lack of concern for religion and weak religiosity, or any act for which a specific punishment is promised in the Qur’an or Sunnah. Then it presents the enumeration according to Abu Talib al-Makki, who categorizes major sins by the body parts involved: four heart sins (shirk, insistence, despair, and security), four tongue sins (false testimony, slander, false oath, sorcery), three stomach sins (drinking alcohol, consuming orphan's wealth, usury), two private part sins (adultery, sodomy), two hand sins (murder, theft), one foot sin (fleeing from battle), and one body sin (disobedience to parents). This enumeration serves as a practical guide to the most serious sins in Islam.

Key Terms
كبيرة
كبائر
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a major sin is simply any great crime in worldly terms.
Correction: The definition is religious: either a lack of concern for religion or a specific threat in revelation, not worldly magnitude.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the best definition of a major sin (kabira)?
A) Any sin that is committed intentionally
B) Every disobedience that indicates lack of concern for religion or has a specific threat in revelation
C) Any sin that is not forgiven
D) Any sin that is committed by a Muslim

Key Takeaway
The best definition of a major sin combines the sinner's disregard for religion and the existence of a specific divine threat in revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you feel a weight in your heart that shows you still care about your religion, or do you find yourself becoming increasingly careless? What does that reveal about your faith?
?
Are there sins you downplay because they have no worldly punishment, forgetting that they may still be considered major by indicating a weak regard for the Deen? How will you change your view of such sins starting today?
?
If the Qur'an and Sunnah specifically threaten a certain sin, do you fear that threat as if it is personally addressed to you, or do you assume it applies to others? What step will you take to make these threats more real in your life?

Abu Talib al-Makki enumerates seventeen major sins grouped by body parts.

Core Idea
Major sins are categorized by body parts, reminding us that every limb can sin.

Having defined major sins, Ghazali now presents a detailed enumeration from Abu Talib al-Makki. This list classifies sins according to the body part involved, offering a practical framework for moral self-accounting.

The Three Degrees of Faith
Ghazali classifies faith into three degrees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of faith into three degrees based on the components of belief, speech, and action, and the Mu'tazila's intermediate position.

Core Idea
Faith is defined by its components; actions are integral.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali presents a nuanced classification of faith according to the presence or absence of its three components: heart belief, tongue testimony, and limb actions. He identifies three degrees, each with distinct rulings, and addresses the Mu'tazila's controversial position on the major sinner. This lays the groundwork for understanding the relationship between faith and works in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three degrees
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It enumerates three degrees of faith based on the presence of belief, speech, and action.
2
Identify the components of faith
Note that Ghazali mentions three components: tasdiq (affirmation) in the heart, qawl (speech) on the tongue, and 'amal (action) of the limbs.
These are standard in Sunni theology.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The second degree involves a major sin. The Mu'tazila place such a person in an intermediate state (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). The author declares this false.
This is a key point of difference.
4
Reflect on the third degree
The third degree has only heart and tongue without actions. The author notes different opinions and introduces Abu Talib al-Makki's view.
This will be discussed later.

بالجوارح .

(۱) واختلف في حد الكبيرة ، وعد الكبائر ، وأحسن ما قيل في حدها هي كل معصية تؤذن بقلة إكتراث مرتكبها بالدين ورقة الديانة ، أو كل ما توعد عليه بخصوصه من الكتاب أو السنة
وأما عد الكبائر : فقد قال الشيخ أبو طالب المكي هي :
أربع من أعمال القلوب : الشرك ، والإصرار ، والقنوط ، والأمن
وأربع في اللسان : شهادة الزور ، وقذف المحصنات ، واليمين الغموس ، والسحر وثلاث في البطن : شرب الخمر والمسكر من الأشربة ، وأكل مال اليتيم ، وأكل الربا وهو يعلمه
وإثنان في الفرج : الزنا ، واللواط ؛
وإثنان في اليد : القتل ، والسرقة
وواحدة في الرجل : فرار الواحد من الإثنين يوم الزحف
وواحدة في الجسد : وهي « عقوق الوالدين » اهـ
(۲) ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر .
(۳) وأبو طالب المكي هو محمد بن علي بن عطية الحارثي المكي ، رحمه الله تعالى ، ذكر ذلك في كتابه قوت
القلوب في الباب الثالث والثلاثين منه .
Whoever combines these three, there is no disagreement that his final abode is Paradise: this is one degree. The second degree: that two and part of the third are present, namely speech and belief and some actions, but its doer has committed a major sin or some major sins (1). According to this, the Mu'tazila said: he has left faith by this and not entered unbelief, but his name is 'fasiq' (wicked), and he is in a station between the two stations (2), and he is eternally in the Fire. And this is false as we will mention. The third degree: that there is affirmation (tasdiq) in the heart and testimony (shahada) on the tongue without actions of the limbs. And they differed concerning its ruling. Abu Talib al-Makki said: (3) (1) And they differed concerning the definition of the major sin and the count of major sins. The best of what has been said in its definition is: every disobedience that indicates the lack of its perpetrator's care for religion and the thinness of piety, or everything that is specifically threatened with punishment in the Book or the Sunnah. As for the count of major sins, Shaykh Abu Talib al-Makki said they are: Four from works of the heart: polytheism, persistence (in sin), despair of God's mercy, and feeling secure (from God's plan). Four on the tongue: false testimony, slandering chaste women, the false oath, and sorcery. Three in the belly: drinking wine and intoxicating beverages, consuming the property of an orphan, and consuming usury while knowing it. Two in the private parts: adultery and sodomy. Two in the hand: murder and theft. One in the foot: the flight of a single person from two at the time of battle. One in the body: it is disobedience to parents. End. (2) Not a believer nor a disbeliever. (3) And Abu Talib al-Makki is Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Atiyya al-Harithi al-Makki, may God have mercy on him. He mentioned that in his book Qut al-Qulub in the thirty-third chapter thereof.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage classifies faith into three degrees and then provides a definition and enumeration of major sins (kaba'ir) as a footnote. The author begins by stating the first degree: one who combines all three components—belief in the heart, speech on the tongue, and actions of the limbs—will have Paradise without disagreement. The second degree involves having two components and part of the third, specifically speech (testimony) and belief, with some actions, but having committed a major sin. The Mu'tazila consider such a person to be a 'fasiq' in an intermediate state between faith and unbelief, eternally in Hell. The author declares this false. The third degree is when only affirmation of the heart and verbal testimony exist, without actions. The author notes disagreement about its ruling and introduces Abu Talib al-Makki, whose view is discussed later. Then, in footnote (1), the author turns to defining the major sin (kabira). He presents a definition: any sin that indicates the perpetrator's lack of care for religion or any sin specifically threatened in the Qur'an or Sunnah. Then he provides a count from Abu Talib al-Makki, dividing major sins by body parts: heart (4), tongue (4), belly (3), private parts (2), hand (2), foot (1), and body (1). This enumeration is comprehensive. The passage thus serves to clarify the role of actions in faith and to categorize sinful acts.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A Muslim who commits a major sin becomes neither a believer nor a disbeliever but is in an intermediate state (Mu'tazila position).
Correction: Ghazali asserts that such a person is still a believer (though sinful) and not eternally in Hell, as will be explained.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the ruling for the first degree of faith?
A) Enter Paradise with no disagreement
B) Enter Paradise only if repentance is made

Key Takeaway
Faith has three components; the second degree involves major sin and the Mu'tazila's erroneous intermediate state is rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three degrees of faith best describes your current spiritual state? What specific component of faith (belief, speech, or action) do you need to strengthen? Commit to one concrete step today to improve that area.
?
Reflect on a major sin in your life. Have you ever used that sin to justify that you are not a true believer? How does Ghazali's rejection of the intermediate state challenge you to take ownership of your faith and actions?
?
The third degree includes belief and speech without actions. In what areas of your life have you been content with just believing and saying the right things, but neglecting to act? What will you do this week to translate your faith into action?

Definition and Enumeration of Major Sins
Ghazali defines and enumerates major sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a major sin (kabira) according to Imam al-Ghazali and the enumeration of major sins by body parts as narrated from Abu Talib al-Makki.

Core Idea
Major sins are those indicating disregard for religion or specifically threatened.

In a footnote to the discussion on faith, Imam al-Ghazali addresses the topic of major sins (kaba'ir). He first gives a definition: a major sin is any disobedience that shows lack of care for religion or is specifically threatened in the Qur'an or Sunnah. Then he provides a detailed enumeration attributed to Abu Talib al-Makki, categorizing sins by the body part involved. This systematic listing aids in understanding the gravity of certain sins and their impact on faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of major sin
Read the first part of the passage where Ghazali presents two criteria for a sin to be considered major.
Note the phrase 'toodhinu biqillat iktirath' indicates neglect of religion.
2
Study the enumeration by body parts
Review the list of major sins grouped under heart, tongue, belly, private parts, hand, foot, and body. Count them.
Total 17 sins according to this list.
3
Reflect on the purpose of enumeration
Consider why the author includes this list: to provide a concrete guide for recognizing and avoiding major sins.
This complements the discussion on faith and sin.

Actions Are Not Part of Faith: Refuting Abu Talib al-Makki
Refutation of Abu Talib al-Makki's view that actions are essential to faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali refutes the claim that actions are part of faith, using the Qur'anic conjunction as evidence that faith and action are distinct.

Core Idea
Actions are necessary for faith, but not part of it.

Ghazali engages with a contemporary scholar's view that actions of the limbs are integral to faith itself. He provides a crisp linguistic argument from the Qur'an that distinguishes between belief and deed. This lesson unpacks that argument and exposes the flaw in claiming consensus.

Guided Path
1
Understand Abu Talib's position
Abu Talib al-Makki claims that actions of the limbs are part of faith and that faith cannot be complete without them. He also alleges that there is consensus on this view.
This is the starting point of the refutation.
2
Examine Ghazali's counterproof
Ghazali quotes the Qur'anic verse 'Those who believe and do righteous deeds' (Surah Yunus 9). He argues that the conjunction 'and' indicates that action is separate from faith, not part of it.
The linguistic point is key: if action were part of faith, the verse would be redundant.
3
Analyze the implication of redundancy
If action were part of faith, saying 'those who believe and do righteous deeds' would be like saying 'those who believe and believe again.' This shows action is external to the essence of faith.
Ghazali uses the concept of 'repetition' (al-mu'ad) to highlight the absurdity.
4
Reflect on the consensus claim
Ghazali notes that Abu Talib's own writings contain evidence that undermines his claim of consensus. The true consensus is that belief without action does not make one a believer in the full sense, but action is still not part of faith's definition.
This step shows the internal inconsistency in Abu Talib's argument.
5
Conclude with the nuanced position
Understand that actions are required for complete faith but are not part of its essence. Faith is belief in the heart and statement on the tongue; actions are their necessary fruit.
This distinction preserves the integrity of faith as a concept while emphasizing the importance of deeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العمل بالجوارح من الإيمان ولا يتم دونه (۱) وادعى الإجماع فيه (۲) ، واستدل بأدلة تشعر بنقيض غرضه ، كقوله تعالى :
الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُواْ الصَّالِحَاتِ ) (۳) إذ هذا يدل على أن العمل وراء
الإيمان لا من نفس الإيمان ، وإلا فيكون العمل في حكم المعاد (٤) ، والعجب انه
ادعى الإجماع في هذا وهو مع
ذلك ينقل قوله .
(۱) وهذا يفهم من سياقه في عدة مواضع منها :
قوله : وان الإيمان والعمل قرينان لا يصح أحدهما إلا بالآخر كما لا يصحان ولا يوجدان معاً إلا بنفي
ضدهما وهو الكفر
وقال في موضع آخر : وقد اشترط الله عز وجل للإيمان العمل الصالح، ونفي النفع بالإيمان إلا
بالعمل
، ووجوده
وقال في موضع آخر : شرط الإيمان العمل والتقوى كما أن شرط الأعمال الصالحة الإيمان وقال أيضا في تفسير قوله تعالى : الْيَوْمَ أَكْمَلْتُ لَكُمْ دِينَكُمْ ) فصارت الأعمال متعلقة بالإيمان وهما الدين المكمل وقال في تفسير قوله تعالى : ( يَقُولُونَ بِأَفْوَاهِهِم مَا لَيْسَ فِي قُلُوبِهِم ) أراد سبحانه أن قول هؤلاء قول المؤمنين وأن قولهم من أعمالهم ، لأنهم متفردون بالقول دون العمل ثم قال بعد ذلك : فإما أن يكون دليلا أن القول حسب هو الإيمان كله ، وأن الإيمان يكون قولا لا يحتاج الى عمل فهذا باطل (۲) وذلك في قوله بعد أن أورد أثراً عن علي رضي الله عنه الإيمان قول باللسان ، وعقد بالقلب وعمل بالأركان ، فادخل أعمال الجوارح في عقود الإيمان، وأيضاً فإن الامة مجمعة أن العبد لو آمن بجميع ما ذكر في عقود القلب في حديث جبريل عليه السلام ، ثم لم يعمل بما ذكرناه من وصف الإسلام بأعمال الجوارح أنه لا يسمى مؤمنا ، وانه إن عمل بجميع ما وصف به الإسلام ولا يعتقد ما وصف الإيمان أنه لا يكون مسلما ، وقد أخبر نبي الله صلوات الله وسلامه عليه أن أمته لا تجتمع على ضلالة ،
فهذه العبارة تشعر بدعو الإجماع . (۳) الآية (۹) من سورة يونس (۱۲) .
(٤) أي المكرر ، وهذا نقيض مطلوبه الذي هو إثبات كون العمل من الإيمان وأنه لا يتم بدونه.
Action by the limbs is part of faith and is not complete without it (1), and he claimed consensus upon it (2), and he adduced proofs that indicate the opposite of his purpose, such as His statement, the Exalted: 'Those who believe and do righteous deeds' (3), for this indicates that action is beyond faith, not from the essence of faith, otherwise action would be in the position of repetition (4). And it is amazing that he claimed consensus in this while he nevertheless transmits his own statement. (1) And this is understood from his context in several places, among them: His statement: 'And faith and action are inseparable; neither is sound without the other, just as they are not sound and do not exist together except with the negation of their opposite, which is unbelief.' And he said in another place: 'And God, Exalted and Majestic, has conditioned for faith righteous action, and negated benefit from faith except with action and its existence.' And he said in another place: 'Faith is conditioned by action and piety, just as righteous actions are conditioned by faith.' And he also said in explanation of His statement, the Exalted: 'This day I have perfected for you your religion': so actions became attached to faith, and they both are the completed religion. And he said in explanation of His statement, the Exalted: 'They say with their mouths what is not in their hearts': He intended, Exalted is He, that the statement of these is the statement of believers, and that their statement is among their actions, because they are singled out by statement without action. Then he said after that: 'Thus it either becomes evidence that statement alone is the entire faith, and that faith is a statement that does not need action, and this is false.' (2) And that is in his statement, after he narrated an effect from Ali, may God be pleased with him: 'Faith is statement on the tongue, belief in the heart, and action on the limbs', so he included actions of the limbs in the contracts of faith. And also, the community is unanimous that if a servant believed in all that is mentioned in the contracts of the heart in the hadith of Gabriel, peace be upon him, then did not act on what we mentioned of the description of Islam by actions of the limbs, that he is not called a believer; and that if he acts on all that is described by Islam and does not believe what is described by faith, that he is not a Muslim. And the Prophet of God, blessings and peace of God be upon him, informed that his community will not gather on error. So these expressions indicate his claim of consensus. (3) Verse (9) of Surah Yunus (12). (4) i.e., repetition, and this is the opposite of his desired conclusion, which is establishing that action is part of faith and that it is not complete without it.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes Abu Talib al-Makki's claim that actions of the limbs are part of faith and that faith cannot be complete without them. The author begins by stating Abu Talib's position and his alleged consensus. Then he provides a counterproof from the Qur'an: 'Those who believe and do righteous deeds' (Surah Yunus 9). The conjunction 'and' indicates that action is separate from faith, not part of it; otherwise it would be redundant. The author expresses astonishment that Abu Talib claims consensus while his own writings show he quotes evidence that contradicts his claim. In footnote (1), the author quotes Abu Talib's own statements showing that he sometimes treats faith and action as inseparable companions, that God conditioned faith with action, and that actions are attached to faith in the verse about perfection of religion. Abu Talib also cites a statement of Ali that faith includes action. Then the author points out that Abu Talib himself argues that if statement alone were faith, it would be false, thus implying that his own position leads to contradiction. The author further argues that the community is unanimous that belief without action does not make one a true believer, and action without belief does not make one a Muslim. This is based on the hadith of Gabriel and the principle that the community does not agree on error. The author concludes that Abu Talib's expressions indicate his claim of consensus, but the author's proof from the verse shows action is separate. The passage is a refutation of the view that actions are essential to the definition of faith, while affirming their necessity.

Misconception: Actions of the limbs are part of the essence of faith, so faith cannot exist without actions.
Correction: Actions are necessary for complete faith but are not part of its definition; faith is belief in the heart and statement on the tongue, while actions are its fruits.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
You know that faith without action is not complete. Is there a command of Allah you have been delaying, hoping that inner belief alone suffices? This lesson calls you to unite belief and deed. What is one act of obedience you have postponed, and what step will you take within the next hour to perform it?
